mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months.    </li>\n          <li class=\"flex items-start\">\n            <span class=\"text-yellow-600 mr-2 mt-1\">3.</span>\n            <span>Refresh every 3-4 days (no exceptions)</span>\n          </li>\n mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months.  </tr>\n          <tr class=\"hover:bg-gray-50 dark:hover:bg-gray-700/50\">\n            <td class=\"p-4 font-medium text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-100\">Environmental Impact</td>\n            <td </tr>\n          <tr class=\"hover:bg-gray-50 dark:hover:bg-gray-700/50\">\n            <td class=\"p-4 font-medium text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-100\">Overall Score</td>\n            <td class=\"p-4 font-bold text-green-600\">9.2/10</span>\n            </td>\n          </tr>\n        </tbody>\n      </table>\n    </div>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. font-bold text-green-600\">9.2/10</span>\n            </td>\n          </tr>\n        </tbody>\n      </table>\n    </div>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months. mb-6\">Why Baking Soda Falls Short for Cat Urine</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Here's the chemistry lesson that explains everything: Baking soda (sodium bicarbonate) is a base with a pH of about 8.3. It works by neutralizing <em>acidic</em> odors. The problem? Cat urine ammonia is <strong>alkaline</strong> (pH 8-9), not acidic.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      When baking soda encounters ammonia, there's no chemical neutralization happening. At best, baking soda can absorb some moisture and provide minimal odor masking. But it cannot chemically bind to or eliminate ammonia molecules.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      That's why in our test, the baking soda box started showing ammonia smell by day 3-4. The soda simply wasn't addressing the actual problem.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Why Activated Charcoal Dominates</h2>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      Activated charcoal doesn't rely on chemical neutralization. Instead, it uses a process called <strong>adsorption</strong>‚Äîwith a \"d\"‚Äîwhere odor molecules physically bind to the carbon's surface.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-6\">\n      One gram of activated charcoal has a surface area of <strong>500-1500 square meters</strong>. That's like a football field of odor-trapping surface in every tablespoon. This massive surface area traps ammonia, hydrogen sulfide, and all the other compounds that make litter boxes smell.\n    </p>\n    <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 mb-8\">\n      In our 90-day test, the activated charcoal box remained virtually odor-free for 18-21 days before needing a refresh. Even then, the odor was significantly less than the baking soda box at day 3.\n    </p>\n\n    <h2 class=\"text-3xl font-bold text-gray-900 dark:text-gray-50 mb-6\">Real User Experiences</h2>\n\n    <div class=\"grid md:grid-cols-2 gap-6 mb-12\">\n      <div class=\"bg-gray-50 dark:bg-gray-800 rounded-xl p-6 border-l-4 border-yellow-400\">\n        <div class=\"flex items-center mb-3\">\n          <span class=\"text-yellow-500 text-lg\">‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÖ‚òÜ‚òÜ</span>\n          <span class=\"ml-2 text-sm text-gray-500 dark:text-gray-400\">Baking Soda User</span>\n        </div>\n        <p class=\"text-gray-700 dark:text-gray-300 italic mb-3\">\n          \"I used baking soda for months.
